var subdomainIndex = siteUrl.indexOf('/');\r\n\r\n            hfSiteUrl.value = location.host + (subdomainIndex >= 0 ? siteUrl.substring(subdomainIndex) : '');\r\n        })();\r\n <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857156 id=\"116857156\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the discovery, spectroscopic confirmation and mass modelling of the gravitationally lensed quasar system PS J0630\u22121201. The lens was discovered by matching a photometric quasar catalogue compiled from Pan-STARRS1 and <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> photometry to the <em>Gaia</em> data release 1 catalogue, exploiting the high spatial resolution of the latter (full width at half-maximum \u223c0.1\u2009arcsec) to identify the three brightest components of the lensed quasar system. Follow-up spectroscopic observations with the William Herschel Telescope confirm the multiple objects are quasars at redshift <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34. Further follow-up with Keck adaptive optics high-resolution imaging reveals that the system is composed of two lensing galaxies and the quasar is lensed into an \u223c2.8\u2009arcsec separation four-image cusp configuration with a fifth image clearly visible, and a 1.0\u2009arcsec arc due to the lensed quasar host galaxy. The system is well modelled with two singular isothermal ellipsoids, reproducing the position of the fifth image. We discuss future prospects for measuring time delays between the images and constraining any offset between mass and light using the faintly detected <em>Einstein</em> arcs associated with the quasar host galaxy.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;methods: observational&quot;\">methods: observational</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;methods: statistical&quot;\">methods: statistical</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;quasars: general&quot;\">quasars: general</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857158 id=\"116857158\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Gravitationally lensed quasars can be used as tools for a variety of astrophysical and cosmological applications (e.g. Treu\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Jackson\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), including: mapping the dark matter substructure of the lensing galaxy (e.g. Metcalf &amp; Madau\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>; Kochanek &amp; Dalal\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Nierenberg et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Vegetti et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Birrer, Amara &amp; Refregier\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>); determining the mass (e.g. Morgan et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and spin (Reynolds et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) of black holes and measuring the properties of distant host galaxies (e.g. Kochanek, Keeton &amp; McLeod\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>; Claeskens et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Peng et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ding et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). In addition, they can also be used to constrain cosmological parameters with comparable precision to baryonic acoustic oscillation methods (e.g. Suyu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and to probe the physical properties of quasar accretion discs through microlensing studies (e.g. Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Poindexter, Morgan &amp; Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Motta et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Ostrovski et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) found that, for the Dark Energy Survey (DES \u2013 DES Collaboration\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; DES Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), no simulated gravitationally lensed quasar system with image separation less than 1.5\u2009arcsec is segmented into multiple catalogue sources due to limitations on survey resolution. As a result, only 23\u2009per\u2009cent of simulated systems with pairs of quasar images are segmented into two sources. This means that to identify lensed quasars as groups of close sources of similar colours, one has to either employ 2D modelling techniques (Ostrovski et\u00a0al., in preparation) or rely on cross-matching to higher resolution imaging surveys such as <em>Gaia</em> (full width at half-maximum \u223c0.1\u2009arcsec; Lemon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em> (Gaia Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016a</a>) is a space observatory performing a full sky survey to study the Milky Way. <em>Gaia</em> data release 1 (DR1 \u2013 Lindegren et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) contains positions for a total of 1.1\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>9</sup> sources across the whole sky with a point source limiting magnitude of <em>G</em>\u00a0=\u00a020.7 (Gaia Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016b</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Here, we present the discovery of the lensed quasar PS J0630\u22121201 from a preliminary search for gravitationally lensed quasars from Pan-STARRS1 (PS \u2013 Chambers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) combined with the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em> \u2013 Wright et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) by cross-matching to <em>Gaia</em> DR1 detections. All magnitudes are quoted on the AB system. Conversions from Vega to AB for the <em>WISE</em> data are <em>W</em>1<sub>AB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>W</em>1<sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0+\u00a02.699 and <em>W</em>2<sub>AB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>W</em>2<sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0+\u00a03.339 (Jarrett et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857163 id=\"116857163\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 LENS DISCOVERY AND CONFIRMATION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To select gravitationally lensed quasar candidates, we first create a <em>morphology-independent</em> (i.e. not restricted to objects consistent with the point spread function \u2013 PSF) photometric quasar catalogue using Gaussian mixture models, a supervised machine learning method of generative classification, as described in Ostrovski et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We use <em>WISE</em> and PS photometry to define <em>g</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>i, i</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>2, <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>1 and <em>W</em>1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>2 colours and we employ three model classes (point sources, extended sources and quasars) to generate a catalogue of 378\u2009061 quasar candidates from a parent sample of 80\u2009028\u2009181 objects with <em>i</em> &lt; 20. To remove stellar contaminants at low galactic latitude, we apply conservative colour cuts based on the comparisons between the known quasar distribution and the distribution of our candidates as well as removal of objects with high point source probabilities. We also discard objects with a neighbouring candidate within 5\u2009arcsec yielding a total of 296\u2009967 objects, since the spatial resolution of PS is enough to flag these systems as potential lensed quasar candidates or binary pairs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We then exploit the excellent spatial resolution and all-sky coverage of the <em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue to identify lensed quasar candidates by resolving the photometric quasars into multiple components. <em>Gaia</em> DR1 is known to be enormously incomplete for close-separation objects but nevertheless can identify multiple components of lensed quasars (see Lemon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a> for details). We cross-match our photometric quasar candidates with the <em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue using a 3\u2009arcsec search radius and find that 1401 of the quasar candidates have two <em>Gaia</em> objects associated with their PS position, whilst 26 of the candidates have three <em>Gaia</em> associations. Visual inspection of these 26 objects revealed one lens candidate, PS J0630\u22121201, shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> with <em>Gaia</em> positions overlaid on the PS cut-out, to be of interest. Its PS catalogue position, and PS and <em>WISE</em> photometry are listed in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. The other objects with triple <em>Gaia</em> matches were ruled out as common contaminants, mainly single quasars with other objects nearby (that, despite being resolved in PS images, were not quasar candidates in our catalogue), as well as apparently faint interacting starbursting galaxies and likely duplicate entries in the <em>Gaia</em> catalogue (objects with separations of &lt;0.1\u2009arcsec).</p>                        <a id=\"116857166\" scrollto-destination=\"116857166\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=h8Y8MVL80-uxQYHFgOZJdpPOcIGTa8NzBSFehD49L340zGaBLpZhe1g-2wJYaq-mONCULRlNU9a5FLVKIX0b9RzDSOcGjLxf6k3qxRFn9Fw-XZQlYnD5LURdwGSTdJTq-Ru5Lk3LzeyhT1A68X24~~FY6dn2MGhHkNT1LRslQdgIpvpG~7dSxqqKno4~SfhsNK0sBhkarG56ic3sbcPMSK7OVB-KnjAXkG61NmnHcAEzIzbITJw6r306Mb8FY02dW7so56v~QLgSiyJVCsN9-rpuGB0tldfwD6On21wGDqIb5rEmEuqEsrgTr8srfFur4nR3eh3B3WV4CFFl-7UJlQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"PS J0630\u22121201 as a g, r, i PS colour composite.        </a></div></div></div><div class=\"table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th>RA<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>06<sup>h</sup> 30<sup>m</sup> 09<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${^{\\rm s}_{.}}$|</span>11<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Dec.\u00a0</td><td>\u221212<sup>d</sup>        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=116857168 id=\"116857168\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1 Spectroscopic follow-up at the WHT</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Spectroscopic follow-up observations to confirm that the multiple components are multiply imaged quasars were performed with the dual-arm Intermediate dispersion Spectrograph and Imaging System (ISIS) on the William Herschel Telescope (WHT) on the night of 2017 April 01 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span>. The R300B grating was employed on the blue arm, providing wavelength coverage from \u223c3200 to \u223c5400\u2009\u00c5 with \u223c4\u2009\u00c5 resolution, and the R158R grating on the red arm resulted in coverage from \u223c5300 to \u223c10\u2009200\u2009\u00c5 with a resolution of \u223c7.7\u2009\u00c5; the 5300\u2009\u00c5 dichroic was used to split the beam to the blue and red arms. Two exposures of 600\u2009s each were obtained with a slit position angle (PA) of 22<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${^{\\circ}_{.}}$|</span>5, i.e. along the direction of the three brightest images, and two clearly separated traces were visible on the red arm, with the two southern-most images not deblended, whilst no clear separation was apparent in the blue data. Extractions of these traces are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>, revealing very similar spectra indicating a lensed quasar at <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34. No features of a lower redshift lensing galaxy are visible in the spectra.</p>                        <a id=\"116857170\" scrollto-destination=\"116857170\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=XjlvGtBYpvVsmi3-n-jxF9RIgIhxVHDk-I5Cqi4lali3Xls4eNZr1Xwmg~~uFUwsCvvdumhSAhpBu~Cwl~Qgoxwh8i6BSkEq8lbaBuyDwwEY45J2Ym3QiFEm51ymKClYOyb~Oo0gM06pNTgQoL1dbsHV1Oy5opTg~bVLjCB8bYgVGil44jNIIe0NuMPLfLKPYlBr73ymkuuN511wdOppE7~winGkYj8SV6P~U9J2UAHB~H9SkIxAEu9J4C3s8-rm0~b9X7WIDp1jZNtuN0riGoXHKkhVzLae7hlLQ1oRs5aOuq7~qrMVqwlloybWpwxzEC3oL50zEaD9eMmfHRwRqg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"WHT/ISIS spectra of component C (black) and the blended components A and B (blue). The NIRC2 narrow camera was used, giving a 10\u2009arcsec\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010\u2009arcsec field of view and 10\u2009mas pixels, and four 180\u2009s exposures were obtained with the <em>K</em>\u0384 filter. These data clearly resolve the three quasars observed with WHT spectroscopy (A, B and C in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>), and also reveal two additional point-like objects (D and E) and two extended objects (G1 and G2). Note that most of the structure around the bright images is an artefact (\u2018waffling\u2019) due to AO correction problems with the low-bandwidth wavefront sensor.</p>                        <a id=\"116857173\" scrollto-destination=\"116857173\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=dTHOko9i20QC5hcHUN73Wfqur1e1EEEPQVagBCidBxMrTu9ogoV1aONxgUE3SnopJT-vJpn8TB3cd8jNIIyFye8YmD1Us92EjI-FIbyNZoF8N~Vc1vKk1tYA-B1JkvubaoTy0vosl2Z4vwxn4GoqNTwKF9ASaFDdSZbJWfQUf~6I4UdvCu1ScHZOJ-VkqSp~747Tgso-o-G9LsUApYnhyREQRBspmpLfN63QzNdmAulfGokN8gx~G7NkavH~9SCz7xj3Y7Nqd-bC3N4HofF4ZWgKi5D-kWH1l7IDZQdC7LeHpgNct45baMvO6-5IyqSagA0KibvhPamQOU7zeyY4rw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Left: NIRC2 K\u0384 AO imaging of PS J0630\u22121201. The additional structure around the images A, B and C is due to poor wavefront correction. Middle: the same as the left-hand panel, but with a model for the AO PSF subtracted from the three brightest images, revealing the presence of a faint arc. Right: same as the middle but with enhanced contrast to better show the lensed quasar host galaxy.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The PSF of image C appears to have a structure extending down from the core of the PSF that is not seen in images A or B but could be consistent with a lensed arc. We therefore produced a pixellated model of the PSF around the images ABC to remove it and increase the dynamic range of the image. In the first iteration of this procedure, an arc between images B and C was clearly visible, and we therefore re-fitted for the PSF excluding pixels that contain arc flux. The residuals of this fit are shown in the middle and right-hand panels of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857175 id=\"116857175\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 LENS MASS MODELLING</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The AO imaging data show that PS J0630\u22121201 is a lensed quasar in a \u2018cusp\u2019 configuration, with two lensing galaxies. The presence of a fifth point source, E in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, is intriguing as it is located approximately where the fifth (demagnified) image would be expected to appear. To understand the nature of E as a possible fifth quasar image, we initially create a mass model using the positions of the brighter quasar images (A\u2013D) and the two lensing galaxies, and use this model to predict the location of any additional images. We first determine the position of each point source by modelling them with Gaussian or Moffat profiles, and we fit S\u00e9rsic profiles to G1 and G2. We also perform moment-based centroiding in a range of apertures, and use the spread of all of our measurements to estimate the uncertainties on the positions; these are typically \u223c1 mas for the point sources and \u223c10\u201320 mas for the galaxies. However, we also find that the relative positions of the quasar images change from exposure to exposure, presumably due to atmospheric fluctuations, and we therefore impose a 5 mas minimum uncertainty on each position. We simultaneously fit the PS imaging data (<em>grizY</em>) whilst fitting the Keck data (<em>K</em>\u0384), and our photometric and astrometric results are given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"116857177\" scrollto-destination=\"116857177\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl2\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl2\" data-id=\"tbl2\"><span class=\"label\">Table 2.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Relative astrometry and photometry of lens components. <em>grizYK</em>\u0384 of D and E are combined with G2 and G1, respectively, due to blending.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>r</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>i</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>z</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>Y</em><span .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u0394\u03b1<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u0394\u03b4<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n \u00b1 0.01\u00a0</td><td>0.000\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.013\u00a0</td><td>0.000\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.010\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>G2\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>20.93 \u00b1 0.01\u00a0</td><td>\u22120.650\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.017\u00a0</td><td>0.862\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.005\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-wrap-foot\"><span id=\"fn-tblf1\"></span><div content-id=\"tblf1\"><span class=\"fn\"><p class=\"chapter-para\"><sup><em>a</em></sup><em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue <em>G</em>-band photometry for components A,B and C.</p></span></div></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857177\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We use the positions of the galaxies and point sources to constrain a lensing mass model. The two galaxies are initially modelled as singular isothermal spheres and we use the positions of the four brighter images to infer their <em>Einstein</em> radii using the <span class=\"small-caps\">lensmodel</span> software (Keeton\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>). The best-fitting lens model predicts a fifth image near the location of image E with a flux approximately half of image D, comparable to the observed flux ratio. We consequently use the position of E to constrain a more realistic lens model, allowing the two galaxies to have some ellipticity, and we include an external shear. This model has five free parameters for each galaxy (two position parameters, an <em>Einstein</em> radius and an ellipticity with its orientation) and two additional parameters each for the position of the source and the external shear, i.e. there are 14 total parameters. We likewise have 14 constraints from the observed positions of the five quasar images and the two lensing galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using image plane modelling, we sample the parameter space using <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> (Foreman-Mackey et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and, as expected, find a best-fitting model with \u03c7<sup>2</sup>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00. Both mass components are inferred to be coincident with the light, with <em>Einstein</em> radii for G1 and G2 of 1.01\u2009arcsec \u00b1 0.01 and 0.58\u2009arcsec \u00b1 0.01, respectively. Both masses are also mildly flattened, but we find that the light and mass <em>orientations</em>, given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, are misaligned, significantly so for G2. We also note that the shear is well constrained (\u03b3\u00a0=\u00a00.14\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01 with PA \u221276) and is particularly large, though there is no nearby galaxy along the shear direction. The total magnification for the system is \u223c53.</p>                        <a id=\"116857180\" scrollto-destination=\"116857180\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl3\" class=\"table-modal class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>PA (light)<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>G1\u00a0</td><td>\u22129\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0</td><td>0.81\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0</td><td>\u221221\u00a0\u00b1\u00a06\u00a0</td><td>0.81\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>G2\u00a0</td><td>\u221269\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0</td><td>0.79\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0</td><td>\u22122\u00a0\u00b1\u00a07\u00a0</td><td>0.83\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.04\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857180\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857181 id=\"116857181\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have presented spectroscopic and imaging data that confirm that PS J0630\u22121201 is a quasar at <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34 lensed into five images by two lensing galaxies. We are able to fit the positions of the five images well with a two-SIE lens model and recover flux ratios to within 30\u2009per\u2009cent with discrepancies likely caused by microlensing and/or differential extinction and reddening, as evidenced by the strongly varying flux ratio between images B and C from optical to near-infrared wavelengths. However, we find that for both lenses the ellipticity of the mass is not consistent with the ellipticity of the galaxy light (Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>) and the inferred shear is quite large. This could be the result of an additional mass component, e.g. a dark matter halo that is not coincident with either galaxy (e.g. Shu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). In that case, the weak demagnification of the fifth image might indicate that the dark matter halo is not cuspy (e.g. Collett et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), although constraints from the quasar image positions alone are not sufficient to test this. Deeper imaging of the arc of the lensed host galaxy and observations at radio wavelengths, where extinction and the effects of microlensing are no longer important, will help to constrain a more complex model for the mass distribution.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The relatively bright fifth image of PS J0630\u22121201 also presents the possibility of obtaining four new time delay measurements, for a total of 10 time delays. Based upon our current best lens model, these delays should range between 1 and 245\u2009d assuming that the lens redshifts are <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a01 (Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl4\" data-open=\"tbl4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>). Because of the overall compactness of the system and the presence of the two lensing galaxies, it would be difficult to obtain time delays from the fifth image with conventional seeing-limited monitoring programmes. However, if such a campaign observed a sudden brightening (or dimming) event in one of the brighter images, dedicated monitoring with a high-resolution facility (e.g. Robo-AO; Baranec et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) could yield an observation of the delayed brightening of the fifth image.</p>                        <a id=\"116857184\" scrollto-destination=\"116857184\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl4\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl4\" data-id=\"tbl4\"><span class=\"label\">Table 4.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Predicted time delays between the image pairs in PS J0630\u22121201. All values are in days. The values in the top right (bottom left) are for a lens redshift of 1 (0.5). Light arrives in the images in the following order: CABED.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>A<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>B<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>C<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>D<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>E<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>0.9\u00a0</td><td>1.8\u00a0</td><td>243\u00a0</td><td>208\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>0.4\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>2.7\u00a0</td><td>242\u00a0</td><td>207\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>C\u00a0</td><td>0.7\u00a0</td><td>1.1\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>245\u00a0</td><td>210\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>D\u00a0</td><td>97.8\u00a0</td><td>97.4\u00a0</td><td>98.5\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>35\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>E\u00a0</td><td>83.7\u00a0</td><td>83.3\u00a0</td><td>84.4\u00a0</td><td>14.1\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857184\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Several other lensed quasars have been observed to have bright additional images, and these systems typically also have multiple lensing galaxies (with the exception being the radio-loud double PMN J1632\u22120033, which has an observed highly de-magnified central image; Winn, Rusin &amp; Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>). The cluster lens SDSS J1004+4112 (Inada et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) is a quad with an observed \u2018central\u2019 image, but the complexity of the mass distribution makes it very difficult to estimate time delays (Fohlmeister et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>). PMN J0134\u22120931 is a radio-loud quasar that is being lensed into five images by two galaxies (Winn et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, the image separations are very small so measuring time delays \u2013 expected to range from an hour to two weeks (Keeton &amp; Winn\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>) \u2013 will be extremely difficult. B1359+154 (Rusin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) and SDSS J2222+2745 (Dahle et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) are six-image lens systems, and in both cases the lens is a compact group of three galaxies. These lenses have intrinsically more complex mass distributions, but time delays may still be informative for cosmography. However, most of the independent time delays are expected to be less than a day for B1359+154 (Rusin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) and quite long (400\u2013700\u2009d) for SDSS J2222+2745 (Sharon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). PS J0630\u22121201 therefore appears to be the most promising lens for measuring additional independent time delays.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857186 id=\"116857186\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgements</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We are grateful to the STRIDES collaboration for many useful discussions about quasar lens finding. AST-1238877, the University of Maryland, Eotvos Lorand University (ELTE), the Los Alamos National Laboratory, and the Gordon and Betty Moore Foundation.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This publication makes use of data products from the <em>WISE</em>, which is a joint project of the University of California, Los Angeles, and the Jet Propulsion Laboratory/California Institute of Technology, funded by the NASA.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This work has made use of data from the European Space Agency (ESA) mission <em>Gaia</em> (<a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"https://www.cosmos.esa.int/gaia\" target=\"_blank\">https://www.cosmos.esa.int/gaia</a>), processed by the <em>Gaia</em> Data Processing and Analysis Consortium (DPAC, <a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"https://www.cosmos.esa.int/web/gaia/dpac/consortium\" target=\"_blank\">https://www.cosmos.esa.int/web/gaia/dpac/consortium</a>). Funding for the DPAC has been provided by national institutions, in particular the institutions participating in the <em>Gaia</em> Multilateral Agreement.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The WHT is operated on the island of La Palma by the Isaac Newton Group of Telescopes in the Spanish Observatorio del Roque de los Muchachos of the Instituto de Astrof\u00edsica de Canarias.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Some of the data presented herein were obtained at the W.M. Keck Observatory, which is operated as a scientific partnership among the California Institute of Technology, the University of California and the NASA. The Observatory was made possible by the generous financial support of the W.M. Keck Foundation. The authors wish to recognize and acknowledge the very significant cultural role and reverence that the summit of Mauna Kea has always had within the indigenous Hawaiian community. We are most fortunate to have the opportunity to conduct observations from this mountain.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This research made use of Astropy, a community-developed core <span type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnrasl\" /> \n    </div>\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857156 id=\"116857156\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the discovery, spectroscopic confirmation and mass modelling of the gravitationally lensed quasar system PS J0630\u22121201. The lens was discovered by matching a photometric quasar catalogue compiled from Pan-STARRS1 and <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> photometry to the <em>Gaia</em> data release 1 catalogue, exploiting the high spatial resolution of the latter (full width at half-maximum \u223c0.1\u2009arcsec) to identify the three brightest components of the lensed quasar system. Follow-up spectroscopic observations with the William Herschel Telescope confirm the multiple objects are quasars at redshift <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34. Further follow-up with Keck adaptive optics high-resolution imaging reveals that the system is composed of two lensing galaxies and the quasar is lensed into an \u223c2.8\u2009arcsec separation four-image cusp configuration with a fifth image clearly visible, and a 1.0\u2009arcsec arc due to the lensed quasar host galaxy. The system is well modelled with two singular isothermal ellipsoids, reproducing the position of the fifth image. We discuss future prospects for measuring time delays between the images and constraining any offset between mass and light using the faintly detected <em>Einstein</em> arcs associated with the quasar host galaxy.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;methods: observational&quot;\">methods: observational</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;methods: statistical&quot;\">methods: statistical</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;quasars: general&quot;\">quasars: general</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857158 id=\"116857158\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Gravitationally lensed quasars can be used as tools for a variety of astrophysical and cosmological applications (e.g. Treu\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Jackson\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), including: mapping the dark matter substructure of the lensing galaxy (e.g. Metcalf &amp; Madau\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>; Kochanek &amp; Dalal\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Nierenberg et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Vegetti et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Birrer, Amara &amp; Refregier\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>); determining the mass (e.g. Morgan et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and spin (Reynolds et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) of black holes and measuring the properties of distant host galaxies (e.g. Kochanek, Keeton &amp; McLeod\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>; Claeskens et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Peng et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ding et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). In addition, they can also be used to constrain cosmological parameters with comparable precision to baryonic acoustic oscillation methods (e.g. Suyu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and to probe the physical properties of quasar accretion discs through microlensing studies (e.g. Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Poindexter, Morgan &amp; Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Motta et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Ostrovski et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) found that, for the Dark Energy Survey (DES \u2013 DES Collaboration\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; DES Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), no simulated gravitationally lensed quasar system with image separation less than 1.5\u2009arcsec is segmented into multiple catalogue sources due to limitations on survey resolution. As a result, only 23\u2009per\u2009cent of simulated systems with pairs of quasar images are segmented into two sources. This means that to identify lensed quasars as groups of close sources of similar colours, one has to either employ 2D modelling techniques (Ostrovski et\u00a0al., in preparation) or rely on cross-matching to higher resolution imaging surveys such as <em>Gaia</em> (full width at half-maximum \u223c0.1\u2009arcsec; Lemon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em> (Gaia Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016a</a>) is a space observatory performing a full sky survey to study the Milky Way. <em>Gaia</em> data release 1 (DR1 \u2013 Lindegren et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) contains positions for a total of 1.1\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>9</sup> sources across the whole sky with a point source limiting magnitude of <em>G</em>\u00a0=\u00a020.7 (Gaia Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016b</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Here, we present the discovery of the lensed quasar PS J0630\u22121201 from a preliminary search for gravitationally lensed quasars from Pan-STARRS1 (PS \u2013 Chambers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) combined with the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em> \u2013 Wright et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) by cross-matching to <em>Gaia</em> DR1 detections. All magnitudes are quoted on the AB system. Conversions from Vega to AB for the <em>WISE</em> data are <em>W</em>1<sub>AB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>W</em>1<sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0+\u00a02.699 and <em>W</em>2<sub>AB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>W</em>2<sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0+\u00a03.339 (Jarrett et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857163 id=\"116857163\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 LENS DISCOVERY AND CONFIRMATION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To select gravitationally lensed quasar candidates, we first create a <em>morphology-independent</em> (i.e. not restricted to objects consistent with the point spread function \u2013 PSF) photometric quasar catalogue using Gaussian mixture models, a supervised machine learning method of generative classification, as described in Ostrovski et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We use <em>WISE</em> and PS photometry to define <em>g</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>i, i</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>2, <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>1 and <em>W</em>1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>W</em>2 colours and we employ three model classes (point sources, extended sources and quasars) to generate a catalogue of 378\u2009061 quasar candidates from a parent sample of 80\u2009028\u2009181 objects with <em>i</em> &lt; 20. To remove stellar contaminants at low galactic latitude, we apply conservative colour cuts based on the comparisons between the known quasar distribution and the distribution of our candidates as well as removal of objects with high point source probabilities. We also discard objects with a neighbouring candidate within 5\u2009arcsec yielding a total of 296\u2009967 objects, since the spatial resolution of PS is enough to flag these systems as potential lensed quasar candidates or binary pairs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We then exploit the excellent spatial resolution and all-sky coverage of the <em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue to identify lensed quasar candidates by resolving the photometric quasars into multiple components. <em>Gaia</em> DR1 is known to be enormously incomplete for close-separation objects but nevertheless can identify multiple components of lensed quasars (see Lemon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a> for details). We cross-match our photometric quasar candidates with the <em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue using a 3\u2009arcsec search radius and find that 1401 of the quasar candidates have two <em>Gaia</em> objects associated with their PS position, whilst 26 of the candidates have three <em>Gaia</em> associations. Visual inspection of these 26 objects revealed one lens candidate, PS J0630\u22121201, shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> with <em>Gaia</em> positions overlaid on the PS cut-out, to be of interest. Its PS catalogue position, and PS and <em>WISE</em> photometry are listed in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. The other objects with triple <em>Gaia</em> matches were ruled out as common contaminants, mainly single quasars with other objects nearby (that, despite being resolved in PS images, were not quasar candidates in our catalogue), as well as apparently faint interacting starbursting galaxies and likely duplicate entries in the <em>Gaia</em> catalogue (objects with separations of &lt;0.1\u2009arcsec).</p>                        <a id=\"116857166\" scrollto-destination=\"116857166\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=h8Y8MVL80-uxQYHFgOZJdpPOcIGTa8NzBSFehD49L340zGaBLpZhe1g-2wJYaq-mONCULRlNU9a5FLVKIX0b9RzDSOcGjLxf6k3qxRFn9Fw-XZQlYnD5LURdwGSTdJTq-Ru5Lk3LzeyhT1A68X24~~FY6dn2MGhHkNT1LRslQdgIpvpG~7dSxqqKno4~SfhsNK0sBhkarG56ic3sbcPMSK7OVB-KnjAXkG61NmnHcAEzIzbITJw6r306Mb8FY02dW7so56v~QLgSiyJVCsN9-rpuGB0tldfwD6On21wGDqIb5rEmEuqEsrgTr8srfFur4nR3eh3B3WV4CFFl-7UJlQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"PS J0630\u22121201 as a g, r, i PS colour composite.        </a></div></div></div><div class=\"table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=116857168 id=\"116857168\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1 Spectroscopic follow-up at the WHT</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Spectroscopic follow-up observations to confirm that the multiple components are multiply imaged quasars were performed with the dual-arm Intermediate dispersion Spectrograph and Imaging System (ISIS) on the William Herschel Telescope (WHT) on the night of 2017 April 01 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span>. The R300B grating was employed on the blue arm, providing wavelength coverage from \u223c3200 to \u223c5400\u2009\u00c5 with \u223c4\u2009\u00c5 resolution, and the R158R grating on the red arm resulted in coverage from \u223c5300 to \u223c10\u2009200\u2009\u00c5 with a resolution of \u223c7.7\u2009\u00c5; the 5300\u2009\u00c5 dichroic was used to split the beam to the blue and red arms. Two exposures of 600\u2009s each were obtained with a slit position angle (PA) of 22<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${^{\\circ}_{.}}$|</span>5, i.e. along the direction of the three brightest images, and two clearly separated traces were visible on the red arm, with the two southern-most images not deblended, whilst no clear separation was apparent in the blue data. Extractions of these traces are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>, revealing very similar spectra indicating a lensed quasar at <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34. No features of a lower redshift lensing galaxy are visible in the spectra.</p>                        <a id=\"116857170\" scrollto-destination=\"116857170\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=XjlvGtBYpvVsmi3-n-jxF9RIgIhxVHDk-I5Cqi4lali3Xls4eNZr1Xwmg~~uFUwsCvvdumhSAhpBu~Cwl~Qgoxwh8i6BSkEq8lbaBuyDwwEY45J2Ym3QiFEm51ymKClYOyb~Oo0gM06pNTgQoL1dbsHV1Oy5opTg~bVLjCB8bYgVGil44jNIIe0NuMPLfLKPYlBr73ymkuuN511wdOppE7~winGkYj8SV6P~U9J2UAHB~H9SkIxAEu9J4C3s8-rm0~b9X7WIDp1jZNtuN0riGoXHKkhVzLae7hlLQ1oRs5aOuq7~qrMVqwlloybWpwxzEC3oL50zEaD9eMmfHRwRqg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"WHT/ISIS spectra of component C (black) and the blended components A and B (blue). The NIRC2 narrow camera was used, giving a 10\u2009arcsec\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010\u2009arcsec field of view and 10\u2009mas pixels, and four 180\u2009s exposures were obtained with the <em>K</em>\u0384 filter. These data clearly resolve the three quasars observed with WHT spectroscopy (A, B and C in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>), and also reveal two additional point-like objects (D and E) and two extended objects (G1 and G2). Note that most of the structure around the bright images is an artefact (\u2018waffling\u2019) due to AO correction problems with the low-bandwidth wavefront sensor.</p>                        <a id=\"116857173\" scrollto-destination=\"116857173\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnrasl/473/1/10.1093_mnrasl_slx173/3/m_slx173fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466397&amp;Signature=dTHOko9i20QC5hcHUN73Wfqur1e1EEEPQVagBCidBxMrTu9ogoV1aONxgUE3SnopJT-vJpn8TB3cd8jNIIyFye8YmD1Us92EjI-FIbyNZoF8N~Vc1vKk1tYA-B1JkvubaoTy0vosl2Z4vwxn4GoqNTwKF9ASaFDdSZbJWfQUf~6I4UdvCu1ScHZOJ-VkqSp~747Tgso-o-G9LsUApYnhyREQRBspmpLfN63QzNdmAulfGokN8gx~G7NkavH~9SCz7xj3Y7Nqd-bC3N4HofF4ZWgKi5D-kWH1l7IDZQdC7LeHpgNct45baMvO6-5IyqSagA0KibvhPamQOU7zeyY4rw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Left: NIRC2 K\u0384 AO imaging of PS J0630\u22121201. The additional structure around the images A, B and C is due to poor wavefront correction. Middle: the same as the left-hand panel, but with a model for the AO PSF subtracted from the three brightest images, revealing the presence of a faint arc. Middle: the same as the left-hand panel, but with a model for the AO PSF subtracted from the three brightest images, revealing the presence of a faint arc. The four images ABCD are in a canonical \u2018cusp\u2019 configuration, but note the presence of two galaxies, G1 and G2, as well as the additional point source E. The additional structure around the images A, B and C is due to poor wavefront correction. Middle: the same as the left-hand panel, but with a model for the AO PSF subtracted from the three brightest images, revealing the presence of a faint arc. Right: same as the middle but with enhanced contrast to better show the lensed quasar host galaxy.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The PSF of image C appears to have a structure extending down from the core of the PSF that is not seen in images A or B but could be consistent with a lensed arc. We therefore produced a pixellated model of the PSF around the images ABC to remove it and increase the dynamic range of the image. In the first iteration of this procedure, an arc between images B and C was clearly visible, and we therefore re-fitted for the PSF excluding pixels that contain arc flux. The residuals of this fit are shown in the middle and right-hand panels of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857175 id=\"116857175\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 LENS MASS MODELLING</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The AO imaging data show that PS J0630\u22121201 is a lensed quasar in a \u2018cusp\u2019 configuration, with two lensing galaxies. The presence of a fifth point source, E in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, is intriguing as it is located approximately where the fifth (demagnified) image would be expected to appear. To understand the nature of E as a possible fifth quasar image, we initially create a mass model using the positions of the brighter quasar images (A\u2013D) and the two lensing galaxies, and use this model to predict the location of any additional images. We first determine the position of each point source by modelling them with Gaussian or Moffat profiles, and we fit S\u00e9rsic profiles to G1 and G2. We also perform moment-based centroiding in a range of apertures, and use the spread of all of our measurements to estimate the uncertainties on the positions; these are typically \u223c1 mas for the point sources and \u223c10\u201320 mas for the galaxies. However, we also find that the relative positions of the quasar images change from exposure to exposure, presumably due to atmospheric fluctuations, and we therefore impose a 5 mas minimum uncertainty on each position. We simultaneously fit the PS imaging data (<em>grizY</em>) whilst fitting the Keck data (<em>K</em>\u0384), and our photometric and astrometric results are given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"116857177\" scrollto-destination=\"116857177\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl2\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl2\" data-id=\"tbl2\"><span class=\"label\">Table 2.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Relative astrometry and photometry of lens components. <em>grizYK</em>\u0384 of D and E are combined with G2 and G1, respectively, due to blending.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>G</em><sup><em>a</em></sup><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>g</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>r</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>i</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>z</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>Y</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>K</em>\u0384<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n \u00b1 \u00b1 0.01\u00a0</td><td>\u22120.650\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.017\u00a0</td><td>0.862\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.005\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-wrap-foot\"><span id=\"fn-tblf1\"></span><div content-id=\"tblf1\"><span class=\"fn\"><p class=\"chapter-para\"><sup><em>a</em></sup><em>Gaia</em> DR1 catalogue <em>G</em>-band photometry for components A,B and C.</p></span></div></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857177\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We use the positions of the galaxies and point sources to constrain a lensing mass model. The two galaxies are initially modelled as singular isothermal spheres and we use the positions of the four brighter images to infer their <em>Einstein</em> radii using the <span class=\"small-caps\">lensmodel</span> software (Keeton\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>). The best-fitting lens model predicts a fifth image near the location of image E with a flux approximately half of image D, comparable to the observed flux ratio. We consequently use the position of E to constrain a more realistic lens model, allowing the two galaxies to have some ellipticity, and we include an external shear. This model has five free parameters for each galaxy (two position parameters, an <em>Einstein</em> radius and an ellipticity with its orientation) and two additional parameters each for the position of the source and the external shear, i.e. there are 14 total parameters. We likewise have 14 constraints from the observed positions of the five quasar images and the two lensing galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using image plane modelling, we sample the parameter space using <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> (Foreman-Mackey et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and, as expected, find a best-fitting model with \u03c7<sup>2</sup>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00. Both mass components are inferred to be coincident with the light, with <em>Einstein</em> radii for G1 and G2 of 1.01\u2009arcsec \u00b1 0.01 and 0.58\u2009arcsec \u00b1 0.01, respectively. Both masses are also mildly flattened, but we find that the light and mass <em>orientations</em>, given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, are misaligned, significantly so for G2. We also note that the shear is well constrained (\u03b3\u00a0=\u00a00.14\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01 with PA \u221276) and is particularly large, though there is no nearby galaxy along the shear direction. The total magnification for the system is \u223c53.</p>                        <a id=\"116857180\" scrollto-destination=\"116857180\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl3\" class=\"table-modal class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>PA (light)<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>G1\u00a0</td><td>\u22129\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0</td><td>0.81\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0</td><td>\u221221\u00a0\u00b1\u00a06\u00a0</td><td>0.81\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>G2\u00a0</td><td>\u221269\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0</td><td>0.79\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0</td><td>\u22122\u00a0\u00b1\u00a07\u00a0</td><td>0.83\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.04\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857180\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857181 id=\"116857181\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have presented spectroscopic and imaging data that confirm that PS J0630\u22121201 is a quasar at <em>z</em><sub>q</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.34 lensed into five images by two lensing galaxies. We are able to fit the positions of the five images well with a two-SIE lens model and recover flux ratios to within 30\u2009per\u2009cent with discrepancies likely caused by microlensing and/or differential extinction and reddening, as evidenced by the strongly varying flux ratio between images B and C from optical to near-infrared wavelengths. However, we find that for both lenses the ellipticity of the mass is not consistent with the ellipticity of the galaxy light (Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>) and the inferred shear is quite large. This could be the result of an additional mass component, e.g. a dark matter halo that is not coincident with either galaxy (e.g. Shu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). In that case, the weak demagnification of the fifth image might indicate that the dark matter halo is not cuspy (e.g. Collett et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), although constraints from the quasar image positions alone are not sufficient to test this. Deeper imaging of the arc of the lensed host galaxy and observations at radio wavelengths, where extinction and the effects of microlensing are no longer important, will help to constrain a more complex model for the mass distribution.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The relatively bright fifth image of PS J0630\u22121201 also presents the possibility of obtaining four new time delay measurements, for a total of 10 time delays. Based upon our current best lens model, these delays should range between 1 and 245\u2009d assuming that the lens redshifts are <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a01 (Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl4\" data-open=\"tbl4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>). Because of the overall compactness of the system and the presence of the two lensing galaxies, it would be difficult to obtain time delays from the fifth image with conventional seeing-limited monitoring programmes. However, if such a campaign observed a sudden brightening (or dimming) event in one of the brighter images, dedicated monitoring with a high-resolution facility (e.g. Robo-AO; Baranec et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) could yield an observation of the delayed brightening of the fifth image.</p>                        <a id=\"116857184\" scrollto-destination=\"116857184\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl4\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl4\" data-id=\"tbl4\"><span class=\"label\">Table 4.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Predicted time delays between the image pairs in PS J0630\u22121201. All values are in days. The values in the top right (bottom left) are for a lens redshift of 1 (0.5). Light arrives in the images in the following order: CABED.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>A<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>B<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>C<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>D<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>E<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>0.9\u00a0</td><td>1.8\u00a0</td><td>243\u00a0</td><td>208\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>0.4\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>2.7\u00a0</td><td>242\u00a0</td><td>207\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>C\u00a0</td><td>0.7\u00a0</td><td>1.1\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>245\u00a0</td><td>210\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>D\u00a0</td><td>97.8\u00a0</td><td>97.4\u00a0</td><td>98.5\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td><td>35\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>E\u00a0</td><td>83.7\u00a0</td><td>83.3\u00a0</td><td>84.4\u00a0</td><td>14.1\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/116857184\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Several other lensed quasars have been observed to have bright additional images, and these systems typically also have multiple lensing galaxies (with the exception being the radio-loud double PMN J1632\u22120033, which has an observed highly de-magnified central image; Winn, Rusin &amp; Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>). The cluster lens SDSS J1004+4112 (Inada et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) is a quad with an observed \u2018central\u2019 image, but the complexity of the mass distribution makes it very difficult to estimate time delays (Fohlmeister et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>). PMN J0134\u22120931 is a radio-loud quasar that is being lensed into five images by two galaxies (Winn et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, the image separations are very small so measuring time delays \u2013 expected to range from an hour to two weeks (Keeton &amp; Winn\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>) \u2013 will be extremely difficult. B1359+154 (Rusin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) and SDSS J2222+2745 (Dahle et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) are six-image lens systems, and in both cases the lens is a compact group of three galaxies. These lenses have intrinsically more complex mass distributions, but time delays may still be informative for cosmography. However, most of the independent time delays are expected to be less than a day for B1359+154 (Rusin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) and quite long (400\u2013700\u2009d) for SDSS J2222+2745 (Sharon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). PS J0630\u22121201 therefore appears to be the most promising lens for measuring additional independent time delays.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=116857186 id=\"116857186\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgements</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We are grateful to the STRIDES collaboration for many useful discussions about quasar lens finding. AST-1238877, the University of Maryland, Eotvos Lorand University (ELTE), the Los Alamos National Laboratory, and the Gordon and Betty Moore Foundation.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This publication makes use of data products from the <em>WISE</em>, which is a joint project of the University of California, Los Angeles, and the Jet Propulsion Laboratory/California Institute of Technology, funded by the NASA.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This work has made use of data from the European Space Agency (ESA) mission <em>Gaia</em> (<a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"https://www.cosmos.esa.int/gaia\" target=\"_blank\">https://www.cosmos.esa.int/gaia</a>), processed by the <em>Gaia</em> Data Processing and Analysis Consortium (DPAC, <a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"https://www.cosmos.esa.int/web/gaia/dpac/consortium\" target=\"_blank\">https://www.cosmos.esa.int/web/gaia/dpac/consortium</a>). Funding for the DPAC has been provided by national institutions, in particular the institutions participating in the <em>Gaia</em> Multilateral Agreement.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The WHT is operated on the island of La Palma by the Isaac Newton Group of Telescopes in the Spanish Observatorio del Roque de los Muchachos of the Instituto de Astrof\u00edsica de Canarias.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Some of the data presented herein were obtained at the W.M. Keck Observatory, which is operated as a scientific partnership among the California Institute of Technology, the University of California and the NASA. The Observatory was made possible by the generous financial support of the W.M. Keck Foundation. The authors wish to recognize and acknowledge the very significant cultural role and reverence that the summit of Mauna Kea has always had within the indigenous Hawaiian community. We are most fortunate to have the opportunity to conduct observations from this mountain.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This research made use of Astropy, a community-developed core <span "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018MNRAS.473L.116O", "Tables status": "Complete", "Inspection status": "Incomplete", "Paper Overview": {"abstract": "We report the discovery, spectroscopic confirmation and mass modelling of the gravitationally lensed quasar system PS J0630-1201. The lens was discovered by matching a photometric quasar catalogue compiled from Pan-STARRS1 and Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer photometry to the Gaia data release 1 catalogue, exploiting the high spatial resolution of the latter (full width at half-maximum \u223c0.1 arcsec) to identify the three brightest components of the lensed quasar system. Follow-up spectroscopic observations with the William Herschel Telescope confirm the multiple objects are quasars at redshift z<SUB>q</SUB> = 3.34. Further follow-up with Keck adaptive optics high-resolution imaging reveals that the system is composed of two lensing galaxies and the quasar is lensed into an \u223c2.8 arcsec separation four-image cusp configuration with a fifth image clearly visible, and a 1.0 arcsec arc due to the lensed quasar host galaxy. The system is well modelled with two singular isothermal ellipsoids, reproducing the position of the fifth image. We discuss future prospects for measuring time delays between the images and constraining any offset between mass and light using the faintly detected Einstein arcs associated with the quasar host galaxy.", "start_page": "L116", "authors": ["Ostrovski, Fernanda", "Lemon, Cameron A.", "Auger, Matthew W.", "McMahon, Richard G.", "Fassnacht, Christopher D.", "Chen, Geoff C. -F.", "Connolly, Andrew J.", "Koposov, Sergey E.", "Pons, Estelle", "Reed, Sophie L.", "Rusu, Cristian E."], "issn": "0035-8711", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1709.08975", "Title": "The discovery of a five-image lensed quasar at z = 3.34 using PanSTARRS1 and Gaia", "type_of_reference": "JOUR", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "methods: observational", "methods: statistical", "quasars: general", "Astrophysics - Astrophysics of Galaxies"], "volume": "473", "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018MNRAS.473L.116O", "end_page": "L120", "number": "1", "publication_year": "2018/01/1", "doi": "10.1093/mnrasl/slx173", "journal_name": "Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society", "author_address": ["AA(Departamento de Astronomia, Instituto de F\u00edsica da Universidade Federal do Rio Grande do Sul, 91501-970 Porto Alegre, Brazil;
